<p><b>Comics</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">medium</a> used to express ideas by images, often combined with text or other visual information. Comics frequently takes the form of juxtaposed sequences of <a href="/wiki/Panel_(comics)" title="Panel (comics)">panels</a> of images. Often textual devices such as <a href="/wiki/Speech_balloon" title="Speech balloon">speech balloons</a>, captions, and <a href="/wiki/Onomatopoeia" title="Onomatopoeia">onomatopoeia</a> indicate dialogue, narration, sound effects, or other information. Size and arrangement of panels contribute to narrative pacing. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartooning" title="Cartooning">Cartooning</a> and similar forms of illustration are the most common image-making means in comics; <i><a href="/wiki/Photonovel" title="Photonovel">fumetti</a></i> is a form which uses photographic images. Common forms of comics include <a href="/wiki/Comic_strip" title="Comic strip">comic strips</a>, <a href="/wiki/Editorial_cartoon" title="Editorial cartoon">editorial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gag_cartoon" title="Gag cartoon">gag cartoons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comic_book" title="Comic book">comic books</a>. Since the late 20th century, bound volumes such as <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novels</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comic_album" title="Comic album">comic albums</a>, and <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Tank%C5%8Dbon" title="Tankōbon">tankōbon</a></span></i> have become increasingly common, and online <a href="/wiki/Webcomic" title="Webcomic">webcomics</a> have proliferated in the 21st century.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_comics" title="History of comics">history of comics</a> has followed different paths in different cultures. Scholars have posited a pre-history as far back as the <a href="/wiki/Lascaux" title="Lascaux">Lascaux cave paintings</a>. By the mid-20th century, comics flourished particularly in the United States, western Europe (especially <a href="/wiki/Franco-Belgian_comics" title="Franco-Belgian comics">in France and Belgium</a>), and Japan. The history of <a href="/wiki/European_comics" title="European comics">European comics</a> is often traced to <a href="/wiki/Rodolphe_T%C3%B6pffer" title="Rodolphe Töpffer">Rodolphe Töpffer</a>'s cartoon strips of the 1830s, and became popular following the success in the 1930s of strips and books such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tintin" title="The Adventures of Tintin">The Adventures of Tintin</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/History_of_American_comics" title="History of American comics">American comics</a> emerged as a <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">mass medium</a> in the early 20th century with the advent of newspaper comic strips; magazine-style <a href="/wiki/American_comic_book" title="American comic book">comic books</a> followed in the 1930s, in which the <a href="/wiki/Superhero" title="Superhero">superhero</a> genre became prominent after <a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a> appeared in 1938. <a href="/wiki/History_of_manga" title="History of manga">Histories of Japanese comics and cartooning</a> (<i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a></span></i>) propose origins as early as the 12th century. Modern comic strips emerged in Japan in the early 20th century, and the output of comics magazines and books rapidly expanded in the post-World War II era with the popularity of cartoonists such as <a href="/wiki/Osamu_Tezuka" title="Osamu Tezuka">Osamu Tezuka</a>. Comics has had a <a href="/wiki/Low_culture" title="Low culture">lowbrow</a> reputation for much of its history, but towards the end of the 20th century began to find greater acceptance with the public and in academia.</p>
<p>The English term <i>comics</i> is used as a singular noun when it refers to the medium and a plural when referring to particular instances, such as individual strips or comic books. Though the term derives from the humorous (or <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/comic" title="wikt:comic">comic</a></i>) work that predominated in early American newspaper comic strips, it has become standard also for non-humorous works. It is common in English to refer to the comics of different cultures by the terms used in their original languages, such as <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration">manga</span></i> for Japanese comics, or <i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">bandes dessinées</span></i> for French-language comics. There is no consensus amongst theorists and historians on a definition of comics; some emphasize the combination of images and text, some sequentiality or other image relations, and others historical aspects such as mass reproduction or the use of recurring characters. The increasing cross-pollination of concepts from different comics cultures and eras has further made definition difficult.</p>

</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 262.66666666667px">
<div style="width: 262.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 260.66666666667px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yellow_Kid_1898-01-09.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="795" data-file-width="1152" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yellow_Kid_1898-01-09.jpg/391px-Yellow_Kid_1898-01-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yellow_Kid_1898-01-09.jpg/587px-Yellow_Kid_1898-01-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Yellow_Kid_1898-01-09.jpg/783px-Yellow_Kid_1898-01-09.jpg 2x" width="261"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Yellow_Kid" title="The Yellow Kid">The Yellow Kid</a></i><br/>
<a href="/wiki/Richard_F._Outcault" title="Richard F. Outcault">R. F. Outcault</a>, 1898</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The European, American, and Japanese comics traditions have followed different paths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECouch2000_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECouch2000-1">[1]</a></sup> Europeans have seen their tradition as beginning with the Swiss <a href="/wiki/Rodolphe_T%C3%B6pffer" title="Rodolphe Töpffer">Rodolphe Töpffer</a> from as early as 1827 and Americans have seen the origin of theirs in <a href="/wiki/Richard_F._Outcault" title="Richard F. Outcault">Richard F. Outcault</a>'s 1890s newspaper strip <i><a href="/wiki/The_Yellow_Kid" title="The Yellow Kid">The Yellow Kid</a></i>, though many Americans have come to recognize Töpffer's precedence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xivBeerbohm2003Sabin2005186Rowland199013_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xivBeerbohm2003Sabin2005186Rowland199013-2">[2]</a></sup> Japan had a long prehistory of satirical cartoons and comics leading up to the World War II era. The <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> artist <a href="/wiki/Hokusai" title="Hokusai">Hokusai</a> popularized the Japanese term for comics and cartooning, <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a></span></i>, in the early 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen201041Power200924Gravett20049_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen201041Power200924Gravett20049-3">[3]</a></sup> In the post-war era modern Japanese comics began to flourish when <a href="/wiki/Osamu_Tezuka" title="Osamu Tezuka">Osamu Tezuka</a> produced a prolific body of work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECouch2000Petersen2010175_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECouch2000Petersen2010175-4">[4]</a></sup> Towards the close of the 20th century, these three traditions converged in a trend towards book-length comics: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comic_album" title="Comic album">comic album</a> in Europe, the <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><i><a href="/wiki/Tank%C5%8Dbon" title="Tankōbon">tankōbon</a></i></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[a]</a></sup> in Japan, and the <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novel</a> in the English-speaking countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECouch2000_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECouch2000-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Outside of these genealogies, comics theorists and historians have seen precedents for comics in the <a href="/wiki/Lascaux" title="Lascaux">Lascaux cave paintings</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xivBarker19896Groensteen2014Grove201059Beaty2012Jobs201298_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xivBarker19896Groensteen2014Grove201059Beaty2012Jobs201298-6">[5]</a></sup> in France (some of which appear to be chronological sequences of images), <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">Egyptian hieroglyphs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Column" title="Trajan's Column">Trajan's Column</a> in Rome,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xiv_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xiv-7">[6]</a></sup> the 11th-century Norman <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xivBeaty201261Grove201016.2C_21.2C_59_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xivBeaty201261Grove201016.2C_21.2C_59-8">[7]</a></sup> the 1370 <i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/Bois_Protat" title="Bois Protat">bois Protat</a></span></i> woodcut, the 15th-century <i><span lang="la" xml:lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Ars_moriendi" title="Ars moriendi">Ars moriendi</a></span></i> and <a href="/wiki/Block_book" title="Block book">block books</a>, Michelangelo's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Judgment_(Michelangelo)" title="The Last Judgment (Michelangelo)">The Last Judgment</a></i> in the Sistine Chapel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xiv_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xiv-7">[6]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">William Hogarth</a>'s 17th-century sequential engravings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove201079_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove201079-9">[8]</a></sup> amongst others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xiv_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xiv-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[b]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="clear:both">
<div class="thumbinner">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow: auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tapisserie_de_Bayeux_31109.jpg"><img alt="An extremely long embroidered cloth depicting events leading to the Norman conquest of England." data-file-height="300" data-file-width="30000" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tapisserie_de_Bayeux_31109.jpg/8500px-Tapisserie_de_Bayeux_31109.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tapisserie_de_Bayeux_31109.jpg/12800px-Tapisserie_de_Bayeux_31109.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tapisserie_de_Bayeux_31109.jpg/17000px-Tapisserie_de_Bayeux_31109.jpg 2x" width="8500"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tapisserie_de_Bayeux_31109.jpg" title="File:Tapisserie de Bayeux 31109.jpg"></a></div>
Theorists debate whether the <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a> is a precursor to comics.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English-language_comics">English-language comics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: English-language comics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/British_comics" title="British comics">British comics</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_American_comics" title="History of American comics">History of American comics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_comic_book" title="American comic book">American comic book</a></div>
<p>Illustrated humour periodicals were popular in 19th-century Britain, the earliest of which was the short-lived <i><a href="/wiki/The_Glasgow_Looking_Glass" title="The Glasgow Looking Glass">The Glasgow Looking Glass</a></i> in 1825. The most popular was <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarkClark199117_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarkClark199117-12">[10]</a></sup> which popularized the term <i>cartoon</i> for its humorous caricatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177-13">[11]</a></sup> On occasion the cartoons in these magazines appeared in sequences;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarkClark199117_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarkClark199117-12">[10]</a></sup> the character <a href="/wiki/Ally_Sloper" title="Ally Sloper">Ally Sloper</a> featured in the earliest serialized comic strip when the character began to feature in its own weekly magazine in 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeskinCook2012xxii_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeskinCook2012xxii-14">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>American comics developed out of such magazines as <i><a href="/wiki/Puck_(magazine)" title="Puck (magazine)">Puck</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Judge_(magazine)" title="Judge (magazine)">Judge</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i>. The success of illustrated humour supplements in the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_World" title="New York World">New York World</a></i> and later the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_American" title="New York American">New York American</a></i>, particularly Outcault's <i>The Yellow Kid</i>, led to the development of newspaper comic strips. Early <a href="/wiki/Sunday_comics" title="Sunday comics">Sunday strips</a> were full-page<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENordling1995123_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENordling1995123-15">[13]</a></sup> and often in colour. Between 1896 and 1901 cartoonists experimented with sequentiality, movement, and speech balloons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGordon200235_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGordon200235-16">[14]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:608px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg" title="Bud Fisher's Mutt and Jeff (1907–1982) was the first successful daily comic strip (1907)."><img alt="Five-panel comic strip." data-file-height="544" data-file-width="1600" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg/600px-Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg/900px-Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg/1200px-Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg 2x" width="600"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg" title="File:Mr. A. Mutt Starts in to Play the Races 1907.jpg"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bud_Fisher" title="Bud Fisher">Bud Fisher</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mutt_and_Jeff" title="Mutt and Jeff">Mutt and Jeff</a></i> (1907–1982) was the first successful daily comic strip (1907).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shorter, black-and-white daily strips began to appear early in the 20th century, and became established in newspapers after the success in 1907 of <a href="/wiki/Bud_Fisher" title="Bud Fisher">Bud Fisher</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mutt_and_Jeff" title="Mutt and Jeff">Mutt and Jeff</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey199411_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey199411-17">[15]</a></sup> In Britain, the <a href="/wiki/Amalgamated_Press" title="Amalgamated Press">Amalgamated Press</a> established a popular style of a sequence of images with text beneath them, including <i><a href="/wiki/Illustrated_Chips" title="Illustrated Chips">Illustrated Chips</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Comic_Cuts" title="Comic Cuts">Comic Cuts</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBramlettCookMeskin201645_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBramlettCookMeskin201645-18">[16]</a></sup> Humour strips predominated at first, and in the 1920s and 1930s strips with continuing stories in genres such as adventure and drama also became popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey199411_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey199411-17">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thin periodicals called <a href="/wiki/American_comic_book" title="American comic book">comic books</a> appeared in the 1930s, at first reprinting newspaper comic strips; by the end of the decade, original content began to dominate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhoades20082_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhoades20082-19">[17]</a></sup> The success in 1938 of <i><a href="/wiki/Action_Comics" title="Action Comics">Action Comics</a></i> and its lead hero <a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a> marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Comic_Books" title="Golden Age of Comic Books">Golden Age of Comic Books</a>, in which the <a href="/wiki/Superhero_comics" title="Superhero comics">superhero genre</a> was prominent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhoades2008x_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhoades2008x-20">[18]</a></sup> In the UK and the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/DC_Thomson" title="DC Thomson">DC Thomson</a>-created <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dandy" title="The Dandy">Dandy</a></i> (1937) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beano" title="The Beano">Beano</a></i> (1938) became successful humor-based titles, with a combined circulation of over 2 million copies by the 1950s. Their characters, including "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dennis_the_Menace_(UK)" title="Dennis the Menace (UK)">Dennis the Menace</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Desperate_Dan" title="Desperate Dan">Desperate Dan</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/The_Bash_Street_Kids" title="The Bash Street Kids">The Bash Street Kids</a>" have been read by generations of British schoolboys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChildsStorry2013532_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChildsStorry2013532-21">[19]</a></sup> The comics originally experimented with superheroes and action stories before settling on humorous strips featuring a mix of the Amalgamated Press and US comic book styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBramlettCookMeskin201646_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBramlettCookMeskin201646-22">[20]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WonderworldComics3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1699" data-file-width="1200" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/WonderworldComics3.jpg/170px-WonderworldComics3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/WonderworldComics3.jpg/255px-WonderworldComics3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/WonderworldComics3.jpg/340px-WonderworldComics3.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WonderworldComics3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Superhero_comics" title="Superhero comics">Superheroes</a> have been a staple of <a href="/wiki/American_comic_book" title="American comic book">American comic books</a> (<i>Wonderworld Comics</i> <abbr title="Number">#</abbr>3, 1939; cover: <a href="/wiki/Flame_(comics)" title="Flame (comics)">The Flame</a> by <a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The popularity of superhero comic books declined following World War II,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201051_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201051-23">[21]</a></sup> while comic book sales continued to increase as other genres proliferated, such as <a href="/wiki/Romance_comics" title="Romance comics">romance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_comics" title="Western comics">westerns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crime_comics" title="Crime comics">crime</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horror_comics" title="Horror comics">horror</a>, and humour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201049_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201049-24">[22]</a></sup> Following a sales peak in the early 1950s, the content of comic books (particularly crime and horror) was subjected to scrutiny from parent groups and government agencies, which culminated in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Subcommittee_on_Juvenile_Delinquency" title="United States Senate Subcommittee on Juvenile Delinquency">Senate hearings</a> that led to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Comics_Code_Authority" title="Comics Code Authority">Comics Code Authority</a> self-censoring body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201049.E2.80.9350_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201049.E2.80.9350-25">[23]</a></sup> The Code has been blamed for stunting the growth of American comics and maintaining its low status in American society for much of the remainder of the century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201050_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201050-26">[24]</a></sup> Superheroes re-established themselves as the most prominent comic book genre by the early 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201052.E2.80.9355_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201052.E2.80.9355-27">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Underground_comix" title="Underground comix">Underground comix</a> challenged the Code and readers with adult, countercultural content in the late 1960s and early 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201066_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201066-28">[26]</a></sup> The underground gave birth to the <a href="/wiki/Alternative_comics" title="Alternative comics">alternative comics</a> movement in the 1980s and its mature, often experimental content in non-superhero genres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHatfield200520.2C_26Lopes2009123Rhoades2008140_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHatfield200520.2C_26Lopes2009123Rhoades2008140-29">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Comics in the US has had a <a href="/wiki/Low_culture" title="Low culture">lowbrow</a> reputation stemming from its roots in mass culture; cultural elites sometimes saw popular culture as threatening culture and society. In the latter half of the 20th century, popular culture won greater acceptance, and the lines between high and low culture began to blur. Comics nevertheless continued to be stigmatized, as the medium was seen as entertainment for children and illiterates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELopes2009xx.E2.80.93xxi_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELopes2009xx.E2.80.93xxi-30">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novel</a>—book-length comics—began to gain attention after <a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a> popularized the term with his book <i><a href="/wiki/A_Contract_with_God" title="A Contract with God">A Contract with God</a></i> (1978).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010222_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010222-31">[29]</a></sup> The term became widely known with the public after the commercial success of <i><a href="/wiki/Maus" title="Maus">Maus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Watchmen" title="Watchmen">Watchmen</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_Returns" title="The Dark Knight Returns">The Dark Knight Returns</a></i> in the mid-1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008172Sabin1993246Stringer1996262AhrensMeteling20101WilliamsLyons20107_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008172Sabin1993246Stringer1996262AhrensMeteling20101WilliamsLyons20107-32">[30]</a></sup> In the 21st century graphic novels became established in mainstream bookstores<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010210.E2.80.93211_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010210.E2.80.93211-33">[31]</a></sup> and libraries<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELopes2009151.E2.80.93152_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELopes2009151.E2.80.93152-34">[32]</a></sup> and webcomics became common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorne2010209_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorne2010209-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Franco-Belgian_and_European_comics">Franco-Belgian and European comics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Franco-Belgian and European comics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/European_comics" title="European comics">European comics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franco-Belgian_comics" title="Franco-Belgian comics">Franco-Belgian comics</a></div>
<p>The francophone Swiss <a href="/wiki/Rodolphe_T%C3%B6pffer" title="Rodolphe Töpffer">Rodolphe Töpffer</a> produced comic strips beginning in 1827,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xiv_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xiv-7">[6]</a></sup> and published theories behind the form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey2010_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey2010-36">[34]</a></sup> Cartoons appeared widely in newspapers and magazines from the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELef.C3.A8vre2010186_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELef.C3.A8vre2010186-37">[35]</a></sup> The success of <i><a href="/wiki/Zig_et_Puce" title="Zig et Puce">Zig et Puce</a></i> in 1925 popularized the use of speech balloons in European comics, after which Franco-Belgian comics began to dominate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVessels201045Miller200717_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVessels201045Miller200717-38">[36]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tintin" title="The Adventures of Tintin">The Adventures of Tintin</a></i>, with its signature <a href="/wiki/Ligne_claire" title="Ligne claire">clear line</a> style,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScreech200527Miller200718_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScreech200527Miller200718-39">[37]</a></sup> was first serialized in newspaper comics supplements beginning in 1929,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200717_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200717-40">[38]</a></sup> and became an icon of Franco-Belgian comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheobald200482Screech200548McKinney20113_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheobald200482Screech200548McKinney20113-41">[39]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uderzo_dibujando_a_Asterix.jpg"><img alt="A man drawing a cartoon character on a large vertical drawing board" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="851" data-file-width="1280" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Uderzo_dibujando_a_Asterix.jpg/220px-Uderzo_dibujando_a_Asterix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Uderzo_dibujando_a_Asterix.jpg/330px-Uderzo_dibujando_a_Asterix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Uderzo_dibujando_a_Asterix.jpg/440px-Uderzo_dibujando_a_Asterix.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uderzo_dibujando_a_Asterix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
French cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Albert_Uderzo" title="Albert Uderzo">Albert Uderzo</a> draws the character <a href="/wiki/Asterix" title="Asterix">Asterix</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the success of <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i><a href="/wiki/Le_Journal_de_Mickey" title="Le Journal de Mickey">Le Journal de Mickey</a></i></span> (1934–44),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove200576.E2.80.9378_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove200576.E2.80.9378-42">[40]</a></sup> dedicated comics magazines<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214.E2.80.93215Lef.C3.A8vre2010186_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214.E2.80.93215Lef.C3.A8vre2010186-43">[41]</a></sup> and full-colour comic albums became the primary outlet for comics in the mid-20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214.E2.80.93215_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214.E2.80.93215-44">[42]</a></sup> As in the US, at the time comics were seen as infantile and a threat to culture and literacy; commentators stated that "none bear up to the slightest serious analysis",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[c]</a></sup> and that comics were "the sabotage of all art and all literature".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove200545.E2.80.9346_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove200545.E2.80.9346-47">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[d]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1960s, the term <i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">bandes dessinées</span></i> ("drawn strips") came into wide use in French to denote the medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove200551_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove200551-49">[45]</a></sup> Cartoonists began creating comics for mature audiences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1998116Lef.C3.A8vre2010186_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1998116Lef.C3.A8vre2010186-50">[46]</a></sup> and the term "Ninth Art"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[e]</a></sup> was coined, as comics began to attract public and academic attention as an artform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200723_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200723-52">[47]</a></sup> A group including <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Goscinny" title="René Goscinny">René Goscinny</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Uderzo" title="Albert Uderzo">Albert Uderzo</a> founded the magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Pilote" title="Pilote">Pilote</a></i> in 1959 to give artists greater freedom over their work. Goscinny and Uderzo's <i><a href="/wiki/Asterix" title="Asterix">The Adventures of Asterix</a></i> appeared in it<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200721_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200721-53">[48]</a></sup> and went on to become the best-selling French-language comics series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScreech2005204_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScreech2005204-54">[49]</a></sup> From 1960, the satirical and taboo-breaking <i><a href="/wiki/Hara-Kiri_(magazine)" title="Hara-Kiri (magazine)">Hara-Kiri</a></i> defied censorship laws in the countercultural spirit that led to the <a href="/wiki/May_1968_events_in_France" title="May 1968 events in France">May 1968 events</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200722_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200722-55">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frustration with censorship and editorial interference led to a group of <i>Pilote</i> cartoonists to found the adults-only <i><a href="/wiki/L%27%C3%89cho_des_savanes" title="L'Écho des savanes">L'Écho des savanes</a></i> in 1972. Adult-oriented and experimental comics flourished in the 1970s, such as in the experimental science fiction of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Giraud" title="Jean Giraud">Mœbius</a> and others in <i><a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9tal_hurlant" title="Métal hurlant">Métal hurlant</a></i>, even mainstream publishers took to publishing prestige-format adult comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200725.E2.80.9328_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200725.E2.80.9328-56">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the 1980s, mainstream sensibilities were reasserted and serialization became less common as the number of comics magazines decreased and many comics began to be published directly as albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200733.E2.80.9334_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200733.E2.80.9334-57">[52]</a></sup> Smaller publishers such as <a href="/wiki/L%27Association" title="L'Association">L'Association</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaty20079_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaty20079-58">[53]</a></sup> that published longer works<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELef.C3.A8vre2010189.E2.80.93190_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELef.C3.A8vre2010189.E2.80.93190-59">[54]</a></sup> in non-traditional formats<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove2005153_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove2005153-60">[55]</a></sup> by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auteur_theory" title="Auteur theory">auteur</a></i>-istic creators also became common. Since the 1990s, mergers resulted in fewer large publishers, while smaller publishers proliferated. Sales overall continued to grow despite the trend towards a shrinking print market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200749.E2.80.9353_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200749.E2.80.9353-61">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_comics">Japanese comics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Japanese comics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_manga" title="History of manga">History of manga</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="853" data-file-width="600" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg/220px-Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg/330px-Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg/440px-Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rakuten_Kitazawa" title="Rakuten Kitazawa">Rakuten Kitazawa</a> created the first modern Japanese comic strip. (<i>Tagosaku to Mokube no Tōkyō Kenbutsu</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[f]</a></sup> 1902)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japanese comics and cartooning (<i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a></span></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[g]</a></sup> have a history that has been seen as far back as the anthropomorphic characters in the 12th-to-13th-century <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dj%C5%AB-jinbutsu-giga" title="Chōjū-jinbutsu-giga">Chōjū-jinbutsu-giga</a></span></i>, 17th-century <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Toba-e" title="Toba-e">toba-e</a></span></i> and <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Kiby%C5%8Dshi" title="Kibyōshi">kibyōshi</a></span></i> picture books,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199622_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199622-67">[60]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock prints</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> which were popular between the 17th and 20th centuries. The <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration">kibyōshi</span></i> contained examples of sequential images, movement lines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMansfield2009253_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMansfield2009253-68">[61]</a></sup> and sound effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen201042_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen201042-69">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Illustrated magazines for Western expatriates introduced Western-style satirical cartoons to Japan in the late 19th century. New publications in both the Western and Japanese styles became popular, and at the end of the 1890s, American-style newspaper comics supplements began to appear in Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201021.E2.80.9322_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201021.E2.80.9322-70">[63]</a></sup> as well as some American comic strips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199622_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199622-67">[60]</a></sup> 1900 saw the debut of the <i>Jiji Manga</i> in the <i>Jiji Shinpō</i> newspaper—the first use of the word "manga" in its modern sense,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201022_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201022-65">[59]</a></sup> and where, in 1902, <a href="/wiki/Rakuten_Kitazawa" title="Rakuten Kitazawa">Rakuten Kitazawa</a> began the first modern Japanese comic strip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010128Gravett200421_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010128Gravett200421-71">[64]</a></sup> By the 1930s, comic strips were serialized in large-circulation monthly girls' and boys' magazine and collected into hardback volumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199622Johnson-Woods201023.E2.80.9324_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199622Johnson-Woods201023.E2.80.9324-72">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The modern era of comics in Japan began after World War II, propelled by the success of the serialized comics of the prolific <a href="/wiki/Osamu_Tezuka" title="Osamu Tezuka">Osamu Tezuka</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGravett200424_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGravett200424-73">[66]</a></sup> and the comic strip <i><a href="/wiki/Sazae-san" title="Sazae-san">Sazae-san</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacWilliams20083HashimotoTraphagan200821Sugimoto2010255Gravett20048_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacWilliams20083HashimotoTraphagan200821Sugimoto2010255Gravett20048-74">[67]</a></sup> Genres and audiences diversified over the following decades. Stories are usually first serialized in magazines which are often hundreds of pages thick and may contain over a dozen stories;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199623Gravett200413.E2.80.9314_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199623Gravett200413.E2.80.9314-75">[68]</a></sup> they are later compiled in <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Tank%C5%8Dbon" title="Tankōbon">tankōbon</a></span></i>-format books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGravett200414_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGravett200414-76">[69]</a></sup> At the turn of the 20th and 21st centuries, nearly a quarter of all printed material in Japan was comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrenner200713Lopes2009152Raz1999162Jenkins2004121_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrenner200713Lopes2009152Raz1999162Jenkins2004121-77">[70]</a></sup> translations became extremely popular in foreign markets—in some cases equaling or surpassing the sales of domestic comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2010158_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2010158-78">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_and_formats">Forms and formats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Forms and formats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Comic_strip" title="Comic strip">Comic strips</a> are generally short, multipanel comics that traditionally most commonly appeared in newspapers. In the US, daily strips have normally occupied a single tier, while <a href="/wiki/Sunday_comics" title="Sunday comics">Sunday strips</a> have been given multiple tiers. In the early 20th century, daily strips were typically in black-and-white and Sundays were usually in colour and often occupied a full page.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBooker2014xxvi.E2.80.93xxvii_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBooker2014xxvi.E2.80.93xxvii-79">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Specialized comics periodicals formats vary greatly in different cultures. <a href="/wiki/Comic_book" title="Comic book">Comic books</a>, primarily an American format, are thin periodicals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrr200811Collins2010227_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrr200811Collins2010227-80">[73]</a></sup> usually published in colour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrr200810_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrr200810-81">[74]</a></sup> European and Japanese comics are frequently serialized in magazines—monthly or weekly in Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201022_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201022-65">[59]</a></sup> and usually black-and-white and weekly in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199623Orr200810_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199623Orr200810-82">[75]</a></sup> Japanese comics magazine typically run to hundreds of pages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199623_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199623-83">[76]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:608px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg" title="A comparison of book formats for comics around the world.  The left group is from Japan and shows the tankōbon and the smaller bunkobon formats. Those in the middle group of Franco-Belgian comics are in the standard A4-size comic album format. The right group of graphic novels is from English-speaking countries, where there is no standard format."><img alt="" data-file-height="1004" data-file-width="2748" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg/600px-Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg/900px-Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg/1200px-Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg 2x" width="600"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg" title="File:Comics volumes - international comparison.jpg"></a></div>
A comparison of book formats for comics around the world. The left group is from Japan and shows the <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Tank%C5%8Dbon" title="Tankōbon">tankōbon</a></span></i> and the smaller <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Bunkobon" title="Bunkobon">bunkobon</a></span></i> formats. Those in the middle group of <a href="/wiki/Franco-Belgian_comics" title="Franco-Belgian comics">Franco-Belgian comics</a> are in the standard <a href="/wiki/ISO_216#A4_series" title="ISO 216">A4-size</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comic_album" title="Comic album">comic album</a> format. The right group of <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novels</a> is from English-speaking countries, where there is no standard format.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Book-length comics take different forms in different cultures. European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comic_albums" title="Comic albums">comic albums</a> are most commonly printed in <a href="/wiki/ISO_216#A4_series" title="ISO 216">A4-size</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove201024McKinney2011_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove201024McKinney2011-84">[77]</a></sup> colour volumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214.E2.80.93215_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214.E2.80.93215-44">[42]</a></sup> In English-speaking countries, bound volumes of comics are called graphic novels and are available in various formats. Despite incorporating the term "novel"—a term normally associated with fiction—"graphic novel" also refers to non-fiction and collections of short works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsmith200516KarpKress20114.E2.80.936_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsmith200516KarpKress20114.E2.80.936-85">[78]</a></sup> Japanese comics are collected in volumes called <i><a href="/wiki/Tank%C5%8Dbon" title="Tankōbon">tankōbon</a></i> following magazine serialization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPoitras200166.E2.80.9367_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPoitras200166.E2.80.9367-86">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gag_cartoon" title="Gag cartoon">Gag</a> and <a href="/wiki/Editorial_cartoon" title="Editorial cartoon">editorial cartoons</a> usually consist of a single panel, often incorporating a caption or speech balloon. Definitions of comics which emphasize sequence usually exclude gag, editorial, and other single-panel cartoons; they can be included in definitions that emphasize the combination of word and image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey200176_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey200176-87">[80]</a></sup> Gag cartoons first began to proliferate in <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheets</a> published in Europe in the 18th and 19th centuries, and the term "cartoon"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[h]</a></sup> was first used to describe them in 1843 in the British humour magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177-13">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Webcomic" title="Webcomic">Webcomics</a> are comics that are available on the internet. They are able to reach large audiences, and new readers usually can access archived installments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010234.E2.80.93236_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010234.E2.80.93236-89">[81]</a></sup> Webcomics can make use of an <a href="/wiki/Infinite_canvas" title="Infinite canvas">infinite canvas</a>—meaning they are not constrained by size or dimensions of a page.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010234McCloud2000222_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010234McCloud2000222-90">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some consider <a href="/wiki/Storyboard" title="Storyboard">storyboards</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhoades200838_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhoades200838-91">[83]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Wordless_novel" title="Wordless novel">wordless novels</a> to be comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeron.C3.A42008225_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeron.C3.A42008225-92">[84]</a></sup> Film studios, especially in animation, often use sequences of images as guides for film sequences. These storyboards are not intended as an end product and are rarely seen by the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhoades200838_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhoades200838-91">[83]</a></sup> Wordless novels are books which use sequences of captionless images to deliver a narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen1977181_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen1977181-93">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comics_studies">Comics studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Comics studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Comics_studies" title="Comics studies">Comics studies</a></div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"Comics ... are sometimes four-legged and sometimes two-legged and sometimes fly and sometimes don't ... to employ a metaphor as mixed as the medium itself, defining comics entails cutting a Gordian-knotted enigma wrapped in a mystery ..."</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><a href="/wiki/R._C._Harvey" title="R. C. Harvey">R. C. Harvey</a>, 2001<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey200176_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey200176-87">[80]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Similar to the problems of defining literature and film,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012128.E2.80.94129_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012128.E2.80.94129-94">[86]</a></sup> no consensus has been reached on a definition of the comics medium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012124_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012124-95">[87]</a></sup> and attempted definitions and descriptions have fallen prey to numerous exceptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126-96">[88]</a></sup> Theorists such as Töpffer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2010158_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2010158-97">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/R._C._Harvey" title="R. C. Harvey">R. C. Harvey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaty201265_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaty201265-98">[90]</a></sup> David Carrier,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126.2C_131_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126.2C_131-99">[91]</a></sup> Alain Rey,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012124_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012124-95">[87]</a></sup> and Lawrence Grove emphasize the combination of text and images,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove201017.E2.80.9319_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove201017.E2.80.9319-100">[92]</a></sup> though there are prominent examples of pantomime comics throughout its history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126-96">[88]</a></sup> Other critics, such as Thierry Groensteen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove201017.E2.80.9319_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove201017.E2.80.9319-100">[92]</a></sup> and Scott McCloud, have emphasized the primacy of sequences of images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2010157.2C_170_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2010157.2C_170-101">[93]</a></sup> Towards the close of the 20th century, different cultures' discoveries of each other's comics traditions, the rediscovery of forgotten early comics forms, and the rise of new forms made defining comics a more complicated task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012112.E2.80.93113_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012112.E2.80.93113-102">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>European comics studies began with Töpffer's theories of his own work in the 1840s, which emphasized panel transitions and the visual–verbal combination. No further progress was made until the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller2007101_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller2007101-103">[95]</a></sup> Pierre Fresnault-Deruelle then took a <a href="/wiki/Semiotics" title="Semiotics">semiotics</a> approach to the study of comics, analyzing text–image relations, page-level image relations, and image discontinuities, or what Scott McCloud later dubbed "closure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012112_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012112-104">[96]</a></sup> In 1987, Henri Vanlier introduced the term <i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">multicadre</span></i>, or "multiframe", to refer to the comics page as a semantic unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012113_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012113-105">[97]</a></sup> By the 1990s, theorists such as <a href="/wiki/Beno%C3%AEt_Peeters" title="Benoît Peeters">Benoît Peeters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thierry_Groensteen" title="Thierry Groensteen">Thierry Groensteen</a> turned attention to artists' <a href="/wiki/Poiesis" title="Poiesis">poïetic</a> creative choices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012112_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012112-104">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thierry_Smolderen" title="Thierry Smolderen">Thierry Smolderen</a> and Harry Morgan have held relativistic views of the definition of comics, a medium that has taken various, equally valid forms over its history. Morgan sees comics as a subset of "<i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">les littératures dessinées</span></i>" (or "drawn literatures").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012112.E2.80.93113_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012112.E2.80.93113-102">[94]</a></sup> French theory has come to give special attention to the page, in distinction from American theories such as McCloud's which focus on panel-to-panel transitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012113_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012113-105">[97]</a></sup> Since the mid-2000s, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Cohn" title="Neil Cohn">Neil Cohn</a> has begun analyzing how comics are understood using tools from cognitive science, extending beyond theory by using actual psychological and neuroscience experiments. This work has argued that sequential images and page layouts both use separate rule-bound "grammars" to be understood that extend beyond panel-to-panel transitions and categorical distinctions of types of layouts, and that the brain's comprehension of comics is similar to comprehending other domains, such as language and music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohn2013_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohn2013-106">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historical narratives of <i>manga</i> tend to focus either on its recent, post-WWII history, or on attempts to demonstrates deep roots in the past, such as to the <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration">Chōjū-jinbutsu-giga</span></i> picture scroll of the 12th and 13th centuries, or the early 19th-century <i>Hokusai Manga</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart201428.E2.80.9329_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart201428.E2.80.9329-107">[99]</a></sup> The first historical overview of Japanese comics was Seiki Hosokibara's <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><i>Nihon Manga-Shi</i></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[i]</a></sup> in 1924.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201023Stewart201429_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201023Stewart201429-109">[100]</a></sup> Early post-war Japanese criticism was mostly of a left-wing political nature until the 1986 publication for Tomofusa Kure's <i>Modern Manga: The Complete Picture</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[j]</a></sup> which de-emphasized politics in favour of formal aspects, such as structure and a "grammar" of comics. The field of <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><i>manga</i></span> studies increased rapidly, with numerous books on the subject appearing in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsella200096.E2.80.9397_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsella200096.E2.80.9397-110">[101]</a></sup> Formal theories of <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration">manga</span></i> have focused on developing a "manga expression theory",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[k]</a></sup> with emphasis on spatial relationships in the structure of images on the page, distinguishing the medium from film or literature, in which the flow of time is the basic organizing element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsella2000100_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsella2000100-112">[102]</a></sup> Comics studies courses have proliferated at Japanese universities, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Japan_Society_for_Studies_in_Cartoon_and_Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Japan Society for Studies in Cartoon and Comics (page does not exist)">Japan Society for Studies in Cartoon and Comics</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E6%97%A5%E6%9C%AC%E3%83%9E%E3%83%B3%E3%82%AC%E5%AD%A6%E4%BC%9A" title="ja:日本マンガ学会">ja</a>)</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[l]</a></sup> was established in 2001 to promote comics scholarship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorita201037.E2.80.9338_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorita201037.E2.80.9338-115">[103]</a></sup> The publication of <a href="/wiki/Frederik_L._Schodt" title="Frederik L. Schodt">Frederik L. Schodt</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Manga!_Manga!_The_World_of_Japanese_Comics" title="Manga! Manga! The World of Japanese Comics">Manga! Manga! The World of Japanese Comics</a></i> in 1983 led to the spread of use of the word <i>manga</i> outside Japan to mean "Japanese comics" or "Japanese-style comics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart201430_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart201430-116">[104]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:208px;max-width:208px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Will_Eisner.jpg"><img alt="An elderly man wearing glasses with a microphone in front of him on the left side." data-file-height="318" data-file-width="240" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Will_Eisner.jpg/100px-Will_Eisner.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Will_Eisner.jpg/150px-Will_Eisner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Will_Eisner.jpg/200px-Will_Eisner.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG"><img alt="A middle-aged man seated behind a table, facing the camera." data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2048" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG/100px-Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG/150px-Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG/200px-Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG 2x" width="100"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a> <i>(left)</i> and <a href="/wiki/Scott_McCloud" title="Scott McCloud">Scott McCloud</a> have proposed influential and controversial definitions of comics.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coulton_Waugh" title="Coulton Waugh">Coulton Waugh</a> attempted the first comprehensive history of American comics with <i>The Comics</i> (1947).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInge1989214_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInge1989214-117">[105]</a></sup> Will Eisner's <i><a href="/wiki/Comics_and_Sequential_Art" title="Comics and Sequential Art">Comics and Sequential Art</a></i> (1985) and <a href="/wiki/Scott_McCloud" title="Scott McCloud">Scott McCloud</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Understanding_Comics" title="Understanding Comics">Understanding Comics</a></i> (1993) were early attempts in English to formalize the study of comics. David Carrier's <i>The Aesthetics of Comics</i> (2000) was the first full-length treatment of comics from a philosophical perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeskinCook2012xxix_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeskinCook2012xxix-118">[106]</a></sup> Prominent American attempts at definitions of comics include Eisner's, McCloud's, and Harvey's. Eisner described what he called "<a href="/wiki/Sequential_art" title="Sequential art">sequential art</a>" as "the arrangement of pictures or images and words to narrate a story or dramatize an idea";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYuan2011Eisner19855_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYuan2011Eisner19855-119">[107]</a></sup> Scott McCloud defined comics as "juxtaposed pictorial and other images in deliberate sequence, intended to convey information and/or to produce an aesthetic response in the viewer",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKovacsMarshall201110Holbo201213Harvey20101Beaty20126McCloud19939_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKovacsMarshall201110Holbo201213Harvey20101Beaty20126McCloud19939-120">[108]</a></sup> a strictly formal definition which detached comics from its historical and cultural trappings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaty201267_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaty201267-121">[109]</a></sup> R. C. Harvey defined comics as "pictorial narratives or expositions in which words (often lettered into the picture area within speech balloons) usually contribute to the meaning of the pictures and vice versa".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChute20107Harvey200176_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChute20107Harvey200176-122">[110]</a></sup> Each definition has had its detractors. Harvey saw McCloud's definition as excluding single-panel cartoons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey20101_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey20101-123">[111]</a></sup> and objected to McCloud's de-emphasizing verbal elements, insisting "the essential characteristic of comics is the incorporation of verbal content".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012113_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012113-105">[97]</a></sup> Aaron Meskin saw McCloud's theories as an artificial attempt to legitimize the place of comics in art history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaty201265_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaty201265-98">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cross-cultural study of comics is complicated by the great difference in meaning and scope of the words for "comics" in different languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorita201033_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorita201033-124">[112]</a></sup> The French term for comics, <i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">bandes dessinées</span></i> ("drawn strip") emphasizes the juxtaposition of drawn images as a defining factor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012130Morita201033_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012130Morita201033-125">[113]</a></sup> which can imply the exclusion of even photographic comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012130_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012130-126">[114]</a></sup> The term <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration">manga</span></i> is used in Japanese to indicate all forms of comics, cartooning,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods2010336_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods2010336-127">[115]</a></sup> and caricature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorita201033_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorita201033-124">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_comics_terminology" title="Glossary of comics terminology">Glossary of comics terminology</a></div>
<p>The term <i>comics</i> refers to the comics medium when used as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uncountable_noun" title="Uncountable noun">uncountable noun</a> and thus takes the singular: "comics <i>is</i> a medium" rather than "comics <i>are</i> a medium". When <i>comic</i> appears as a countable noun it refers to instances of the medium, such as individual comic strips or comic books: "Tom's comics <i>are</i> in the basement."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman20128ChuteDeKoven2012175Fingeroth20084_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman20128ChuteDeKoven2012175Fingeroth20084-128">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Panels are individual images containing a segment of action,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee197815_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee197815-129">[117]</a></sup> often surrounded by a border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEisner198528.2C_45_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEisner198528.2C_45-130">[118]</a></sup> Prime moments in a narrative are broken down into panels via a process called encapsulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith200910_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith200910-131">[119]</a></sup> The reader puts the pieces together via the process of closure by using background knowledge and an understanding of panel relations to combine panels mentally into events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009316_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009316-132">[120]</a></sup> The size, shape, and arrangement of panels each affect the timing and pacing of the narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEisner198530_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEisner198530-133">[121]</a></sup> The contents of a panel may be asynchronous, with events depicted in the same image not necessarily occurring at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009315KarpKress201112.E2.80.9313_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009315KarpKress201112.E2.80.9313-134">[122]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg"><img alt="A comics panel.  In the top left, a caption with a yellow background reads, &quot;Suddenly the street is filled with angry people!&quot;  In the main panel, anthropomorphic characters crowd a sidewalk.  A monkey, standing to the left on the road beside the curb, says, &quot;Gosh!  Where'd all these people come from?&quot;  An overweight male on the sidewalk in the middle facing right says to a police officer, &quot;Hey!  My watch disappeared from my parlor!&quot;  An female near the bottom right, says to a male in the bottom right corner, &quot;My necklace!  It's gone from the table!!&quot;" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="446" data-file-width="661" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg/220px-PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg/330px-PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg/440px-PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A caption (the yellow box) gives the narrator a voice. The characters' dialogue appears in <a href="/wiki/Speech_balloon" title="Speech balloon">speech balloons</a>. The tail of the balloon indicates the speaker.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Text is frequently incorporated into comics via <a href="/wiki/Speech_balloon" title="Speech balloon">speech balloons</a>, captions, and sound effects. Speech balloons indicate dialogue (or thought, in the case of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thought_balloon" title="Thought balloon">thought balloons</a>), with tails pointing at their respective speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee197815Markstein2010Eisner1985157Dawson2010112Saraceni20039_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee197815Markstein2010Eisner1985157Dawson2010112Saraceni20039-135">[123]</a></sup> Captions can give voice to a narrator, convey characters' dialogue or thoughts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee197815LygaLyga2004_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee197815LygaLyga2004-136">[124]</a></sup> or indicate place or time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaraceni20039KarpKress201118_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaraceni20039KarpKress201118-137">[125]</a></sup> Speech balloons themselves are strongly associated with comics, such that the addition of one to an image is sufficient to turn the image into comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForcevilleVealeFeyaerts201056_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForcevilleVealeFeyaerts201056-138">[126]</a></sup> Sound effects mimic non-vocal sounds textually using <a href="/wiki/Onomatopoeia" title="Onomatopoeia">onomatopoeia</a> sound-words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009156.2C_318_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009156.2C_318-139">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartooning" title="Cartooning">Cartooning</a> is most frequently used in making comics, traditionally using ink (especially <a href="/wiki/India_ink" title="India ink">India ink</a>) with <a href="/wiki/Dip_pen" title="Dip pen">dip pens</a> or ink brushes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkstein2010LygaLyga2004161Lee1978145Rhoades2008139_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkstein2010LygaLyga2004161Lee1978145Rhoades2008139-140">[128]</a></sup> mixed media and digital technology have become common. Cartooning techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Motion_lines" title="Motion lines">motion lines</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBramlett201225Guigar2010126Cates201098_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBramlett201225Guigar2010126Cates201098-141">[129]</a></sup> and abstract symbols are often employed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsmith200521KarpKress201113.E2.80.9314_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsmith200521KarpKress201113.E2.80.9314-142">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>While comics are often the work of a single creator, the labour of making them is frequently divided between a number of specialists. There may be separate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comics_writer" title="Comics writer">writers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comics_artist" title="Comics artist">artists</a>, and artists may specialize in parts of the artwork such as characters or backgrounds, as is common in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO.27Nale2010384_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO.27Nale2010384-143">[131]</a></sup> Particularly in American superhero comic books,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETondro201151_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETondro201151-144">[132]</a></sup> the art may be divided between a <a href="/wiki/Penciller" title="Penciller">penciller</a>, who lays out the artwork in pencil;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELygaLyga2004161_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELygaLyga2004161-145">[133]</a></sup> an <a href="/wiki/Inker" title="Inker">inker</a>, who finishes the artwork in ink;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkstein2010LygaLyga2004161Lee1978145_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkstein2010LygaLyga2004161Lee1978145-146">[134]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Colorist" title="Colorist">colourist</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009315_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009315-147">[135]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Letterer" title="Letterer">letterer</a>, who adds the captions and speech balloons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELygaLyga2004163_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELygaLyga2004163-148">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The English term <i>comics</i> derives from the humorous (or "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/comic" title="wikt:comic">comic</a>") work which predominated in early American newspaper comic strips; usage of the term has become standard for non-humorous works as well. The term "comic book" has a similarly confusing history: they are most often not humorous; nor are they regular books, but rather periodicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012p._131_.28translator.27s_note.29_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012p._131_.28translator.27s_note.29-149">[137]</a></sup> It is common in English to refer to the comics of different cultures by the terms used in their original languages, such as <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a></span></i> for Japanese comics, or <i><span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">bandes dessinées</span></i> for French-language <a href="/wiki/Franco-Belgian_comics" title="Franco-Belgian comics">Franco-Belgian comics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKinney2011xiii_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKinney2011xiii-150">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many cultures have taken their words for comics from English, including Russian (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">Комикс</span>, <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Russian transliteration"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_comics&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Russian comics (page does not exist)">komiks</a></span></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlaniz20107_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlaniz20107-151">[139]</a></sup> and German (<i><span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><a href="/wiki/German_comics" title="German comics">comic</a></span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrahm2003_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrahm2003-152">[140]</a></sup> Similarly, the Chinese term <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Chinese transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Manhua" title="Manhua">manhua</a></span></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWong200211Cooper-Chen2010177_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWong200211Cooper-Chen2010177-153">[141]</a></sup> and the Korean <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Korean transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Manhwa" title="Manhwa">manhwa</a></span></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods2010301_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods2010301-154">[142]</a></sup> derive from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese characters</a> with which the Japanese term <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Japanese transliteration">manga</span></i> is written.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper-Chen2010177_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper-Chen2010177-155">[143]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cartoon" title="wikt:cartoon">cartoon</a>": from the Italian <span lang="it" xml:lang="it"><i>cartone</i></span>, meaning "card", which referred to the cardboard on which the cartoons were typically drawn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177-13">[11]</a></sup></span></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

(1988). <i>The New Comics</i>. <a href="/wiki/Berkley_Books" title="Berkley Books">Berkley Books</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-425-11366-3" title="Special:BookSources/0-425-11366-3">0-425-11366-3</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AComics&amp;rft.btitle=The+New+Comics&amp;rft.date=1988&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-425-11366-3&amp;rft.pub=Berkley+Books&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
